    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="99df888250d44e838a282f5ac2edb21a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '309197ca-f6e2-47ac-aca3-e762d8df7728'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d39507016" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Both high operation voltage and theoretical capacity promise polyanion-type fluorophosphate Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F as a competitive cathode toward high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, the intrinsic low kinetic characteristics seriously influence its high-power property and service life. To well address this, a creative tiny high-entropy&nbsp;(HE) doping methodology is purposefully developed to fabricate nanoscale Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.94</sub>(Cr, Mn, Co, Ni, Cu)<sub>0.06</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF-HE) as the advanced cathode materials for SIBs. The grain refinement effect induced by collaborative regulations from polyvinyl pyrrolidone and tiny HE heteroatomic doping is reasonably proposed for nanosizing particle dimension of NVPOF-HE. Systematic experiments and theoretical calculations authenticate that the HE doping efficiently promotes the electronic/ionic transport and high-voltage capacity contribution, and weakens the lattice expansion over Na<sup>+</sup>-(de)intercalation processes. Thanks to the appealing virtues mentioned here, the nano NVPOF-HE, compared to single-ion/dual-ion/triple-ion doped cases, achieves even better Na<sup>+</sup>-storage performance in terms of both high-rate capacities and long-term cycling stability. Furthermore, the NVPOF-HE assembled full SIBs deliver a high materials-level energy density of 463&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and electrochemical stability of ≈93.8% capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 5 C rate. More essentially, the fundamental insights gained here provide a significant scientific and technological advancement in high-performance and durable polyanionic cathodes toward next-generation SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403282-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403282-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In the voyage of exploring low-cost, large-scale, and efficient energy storage systems, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted wide attention due to their abundant sodium resources and electrochemical mechanism similar to the current mainstream lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0001_R_d39507008e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0005_R_d39507008e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Storage Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the large Na<sup>+</sup> ion radius causes a series of problems, such as difficult ion migration, inferior redox reaction kinetics, low operating voltage, short service life, etc.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0006_R_d39507008e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Power Sources">6</a>-<a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0010_R_d39507008e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It turns out to be highly necessary to develop and optimize suitable cathode materials accordingly.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0011_R_d39507008e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A large number of positive electrode materials have been developed to date, and the materials with a rigid 3D polyanionic transition metal vanadium-based phosphate skeleton are considered as one of the best options due to their high thermal and structural stability during the cycle. Among them, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) is the most widely utilized for SIBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0012_R_d39507008e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Eng. J.">12</a>-<a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0014_R_d39507008e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Storage Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but the inherent low theoretical capacity due to the huge anion group limits its application, therefore other compounds are urgently needed to expand its universality.</p>
         
         <p>Fluorophosphate Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF), as another typical polyanionic compound composed of a 3D transition metal phosphate skeleton, is theoretically possible to obtain a higher capacity of ≈130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> due to partial replacement of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> by lighter fluoride and oxygen ions, which renders NVPOF more optimistic for SIBs as cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0015_R_d39507008e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The layered skeleton in the <i>a-b</i> plane of NVPOF is typically linked by the VO<sub>5</sub>F octahedra, and the PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra alternatively with sharing cornered-O, and the shared atomic F in the interlayered space acts as the pillars in the <i>c</i> direction. In addition, benefiting from the induction effect derived from the high electronegativity of F<sup>−</sup> and the V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>5+</sup> based redox reaction, the working voltage of NVPOF can reach ≈3.8&nbsp;V, resulting in a high specific energy density of more than 500&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0016_R_d39507008e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Energy">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Its columnar 2D structure and adjustable transition metal central component/coordination means that NVPOF can be designed by rationally adjusting the element ratio, which has gained wide attention. However, owing to the redox and phase transition of transition metal centers during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation process, and low intrinsic conductivity caused by the alternating connection of transition metal octahedrons and anionic tetrahedrons,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0017_R_d39507008e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the high-rate properties of NVPOF are still limited as well as structural stability over cycling.</p>
         
         <p>Generally, multiple strategies including carbon coating,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0018_R_d39507008e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> transition metal doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0019_R_d39507008e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and nanosizing<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0020_R_d39507008e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Storage Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are well-established to solve the aforementioned issues of NVPOF itself, improving electronic conductivity and kinetic properties of redox centers toward efficient yet stable sodium storage. However, carbon coating requires strict control of the content of carbon components, since the redundant carbon content will reduce the tap density of materials, leading to the decline of the energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0013_R_d39507008e619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the doping strategy, the doping effect of a single element is limited, and excessive doping will cause lattice distortion, destroy the stable structure of the material itself, resulting in irreversible phase change and capacity loss even in the initial charge-discharge stage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0021_R_d39507008e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a>-<a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0023_R_d39507008e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nano Lett.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, a concept of preparing advanced NVPOF materials is needed to solve the above problems more efficiently. In recent years, stimulated by the concept of entropy stabilization in metallic alloys, the high-entropy (HE) doping methodology has been extended to various functional/structural materials, and well verified to be beneficial to the structural stability of the matrix, owing to the well-established “entropy-stabilization” effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0024_R_d39507008e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy Mater.">24</a>-<a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0028_R_d39507008e630" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The increase in HE components can lead to a variety of possible combinations of interactions, which are multi-element metal systems that traditional materials cannot achieve with only one or several dominant elements, thus stabilizing crystal structure and ensuring a stable output voltage of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0025_R_d39507008e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides this, the HE doping can redistribute the charge, and further change the electronic state around the Fermi energy level, which can accelerate the electronic conductivity accordingly,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0026_R_d39507008e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> making up for the absence of conductive carbon. Furthermore, the HE substitution strategy compensates for the lack of voltage tunability within the active transition metal centers dictated by the fixed lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0029_R_d39507008e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Nano">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Even for the HE doping, the appropriate doping content still must be taken seriously. In our previous work, the tiny Cr doping (x = 0.06) was identified as an efficient way to improve the comprehensive electrochemical behaviors of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2-x</sub>Cr<sub>x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C (0 ≤ x ≤ 0.1) in both structural stability and rate property for sodium storage, thanks to the striking effect of “four ounces can move a thousand pounds”.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0030_R_d39507008e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Res.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Except for the HE doping, the one worthwhile point is the nano-dimension of active materials, which can minimize the ion diffusion distance, boost electronic conductivity, and increase electroactive sites meanwhile for efficient sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0031_R_d39507008e667" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this work, we purposefully designed a novel carbon-coating-free avenue, i.e., a collaborative strategy of tiny HE doping and nano-engineering, to construct nanoscale Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2-x</sub>(Cr, Mn, Co, Ni, Cu)<sub>x</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (x = 0.06, referred to as NVPOF-HE) as the competitive cathode materials for advanced SIBs. With the fine polyvinyl pyrrolidone (PVP)-involved regulation, the size of resulted NVPOF-HE hugely decreased from several micrometers to nanometers. Further comprehensive physico-chemical/electrochemical characterizations and theoretical calculations evidenced that the tiny V-site HE doping can effectively boost the Na<sup>+</sup> migration rate, optimize electronic conductivity, enhance high discharge capacity in high-voltage region, and restrain lattice expansion during charge–discharge process, thus promoting the high-rate cycling stability under high average operating voltage. Benefiting from the remarkable HE effects mentioned here, compared to the phase-pure NVPOF, single-ion doped Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.94</sub>Cr<sub>0.06</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF-C), dual-ion doped Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.94</sub>Cr<sub>0.03</sub>Co<sub>0.03</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF-CC) and triple-ion doped Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>1.94</sub>Cr<sub>0.02</sub>Co<sub>0.02</sub>Ni<sub>0.02</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F (NVPOF-CCN), the NVPOF-HE cathode achieved even better electrochemical Na-storage properties in term of both high-rate capacity and long-duration cyclic stability. What was more, the NVPOF-HE based full SIBs were assembled with commercial hard carbon (HC) as the anode, and exhibited impressive electrochemical properties including excellent reversible capacity, long-span cycle behavior, and energy density, making an essential step toward its practical industrial application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403282-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403282-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403282-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403282-sec-0030-title">2.1 Morphology and Structure Characteristics</h3>
            
            <p><b>Scheme</b> <a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0008">1</a> illustrates the synthetic process of NVPOF-HE. Typically, the whole process consists two procedures, i.e., hydrothermal treatment and following high-temperature calcination. To optimize the specific synthesis parameters, several parallel experiments were first conducted. Obviously, without the PVP over hydrothermal treatment, the achieved NVPOF sample exhibits micron-scale polyhedral structure (≈10&nbsp;µm, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a,b</a>; Supporting Information), and poor electrochemical sodium-storage behaviors including rapid capacity decay, serious polarization, and low sodium ion diffusion rate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), which, to a large extent, results from its large size at microscale level. For this, its application in SIBs as a cathode is seriously limited. Accordingly, the surfactant PVP is purposefully introduced to restrain the crystal growth of particles, allowing hydrothermal products to be obtained at nanoscale. As expected, the nano-dimensional NVPOF of ≈210&nbsp;nm in size is gained after the addition of PVP in the reaction system (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), and displays enhanced electrochemical kinetics (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) when compared to its micro-size counterpart (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2c</a>, Supporting Information). Note that the high crystallinity of samples favors the improvement in their Na<sup>+</sup>-storage properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0032_R_d39507008e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Eng. J.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, further optimization in annealing temperatures from 450 to 700&nbsp;°C is conducted under Ar atmosphere. Apparently, with the calcination temperature increasing, the diffraction peaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) become even sharper, indicating the improved crystallinity of NVPOF. However, with the annealing temperature increasing up to 600&nbsp;°C, the impurity of NVP phase appears, and turns out to be even more when the temperature reaches 700&nbsp;°C, which is authenticated as well by the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Typically, two pairs of redox peaks contributed by NVPOF gradually became even sharper and more symmetric with increasing calcination temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0026_R_d39507008e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the samples above 600&nbsp;°C, a new pair of redox peaks stemming from the NVP appears at lower potentials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0033_R_d39507008e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Storage Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, the annealing temperature of 500&nbsp;°C is optimized and fixed to avoid the appearance of a low voltage plateaus reducing device-level energy density of cells. All in all, the use of PVP and an annealing temperature of 500&nbsp;°C are chosen for controllably synthesizing the nanoscale NVPOF-HE.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403282-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/af58c4d6-c066-4044-9a3e-598a825305cb/aenm202403282-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/af58c4d6-c066-4044-9a3e-598a825305cb/aenm202403282-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a2634c32-fe41-4225-9283-2f41ff66c00a/aenm202403282-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/af58c4d6-c066-4044-9a3e-598a825305cb/aenm202403282-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403282-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403282" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration for synthetic procedure of NVPOF-HE.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Clearly, all the as-obtained NVPOF-based products (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), regardless of doped or not, show the almost the same reflections within the 2<i>θ</i> range from 10 to 90°, which can be well identified as the tetragonal NVPOF with a space group of <i>I4/mmm</i> (JCPDS no. 97-024-5125).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0034_R_d39507008e821" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such observation authenticates the single-ion/double-ion and/or multiple-ion doping does not lead to any other hetero-phases at all, and typical cubic-structure NVPOF is well retained for all the cases. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the successful X-ray Rietveld refinement of NVPOF-HE, evidenced by a convincing <i>R</i>-factor (<i>R<sub>wp</sub></i> = 8.586%). As noted, the derived lattice parameters of NVPOF-HE (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 6.37271, <i>c</i> = 10.61742) manifest a slight lattice distortion, which will increase Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channel, facilitating the rapid diffusion of sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0035_R_d39507008e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Eur. J. Inorg. Chem.">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0036_R_d39507008e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Mater. Chem. A">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similar phenomena are also found for the cases of NVPOF-C, NVPOF-CC, and NVPOF-CCN (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0001">1b</a> schematically illustrates the crystallographic framework of NVPOF and NVPOF-HE. Notably, after HE-doping, although somewhat shrinkage occurs in the <i>ab</i> plane constructed with HE-VO<sub>5</sub>F octahedra and PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra connected through the shared O atoms, the enlargement of ≈0.051% in <i>c</i>-axis direction can be estimated, which favors for rapid transport of Na<sup>+</sup> in the 3D framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0015_R_d39507008e874" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides this, compared to NVPOF, the introduction of HE doping helps for strengthening the V-O and V-F bonds. The longer bond lengths of V-O2 and V-F in the NVPOF-HE reach 1.614 and 2.106 Å, respectively, effectively reducing the crystal distortion and providing a more stable structure for sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0026_R_d39507008e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0030_R_d39507008e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Res.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The thermogravimetric analysis plot of NVPOF-HE (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrates no discernable mass loss over the testing temperature range of 30–800&nbsp;°C under air condition, persuasively confirming the carbon-free feature of NVPOF-HE.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403282-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/157abf4e-3170-4017-b035-7f58aac41592/aenm202403282-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/157abf4e-3170-4017-b035-7f58aac41592/aenm202403282-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7df44964-ab82-41d6-9e4a-eee0a09963fb/aenm202403282-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/157abf4e-3170-4017-b035-7f58aac41592/aenm202403282-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403282-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403282" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD Rietveld refinement profile of NVPOF-HE; b) schematic illustration for the change in crystal structure from NVPOF to NVPOF-HE; c) V 2p, d) Cr 2p, e) Mn 2p, f) Co 2p, g) Ni 2p, and h) Cu 2p XPS spectra of NVPOF-HE.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Further X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were performed to figure out the chemical states of various metal ions in NVPOF-HE. The XPS survey spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information) reveals the co-presence of dominated Na, V, P, O, and F, along with the doped Cr, Cu, Mn, Co, and Ni. And the atomic ratio of Na to V/P/O/F is very closed to stoichiometric ratio of these atoms in NVPOF-HE. High-resolution V 2p spectrum is fitted as V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (517.3&nbsp;eV) and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (524.8&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0001">1c</a>), suggesting that the V in NVPOF-HE exists in the form of V<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0037_R_d39507008e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typical F 1s spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a>, Supporting Information) shows a unique peak at 684.5&nbsp;eV, revealing the presence of F<sup>−</sup>. And the spectra of P 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and P 2p<sub>3/2</sub> are filtered at 133.9 and 133.0&nbsp;eV, respectively, in the P 2p spectrum (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b</a>, Supporting Information), are related to the binding energies of metal phosphates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0038_R_d39507008e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Sci. Bull.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides the typical P-O bond at 531.2&nbsp;eV, the peak centering at 532.8&nbsp;eV, corresponding to oxygen vacancies (<i>O<sub>vac</sub></i>), is also observed in the high-resolution XPS spectrum of O 1s (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c</a>, Supporting Information). The spectrograph of Cr 2p consists of Cr 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (578.3&nbsp;eV) and Cr 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (583.1&nbsp;eV), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0001">1d</a>, indicating the Cr of a trivalent state existing in the sample.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0039_R_d39507008e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - ACS Nano">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Mn 2p (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0001">1e</a>), Co 2p (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0001">1f</a>), Ni 2p (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0001">1g</a>), and Cu 2p (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0001">1h</a>) are perfectly fitted as follows: Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (641.7&nbsp;eV) and Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (654&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0040_R_d39507008e982" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - ACS Energy Lett.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (781.2&nbsp;eV) and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (797.0&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0041_R_d39507008e992" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Ceram. Int.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (856.6&nbsp;eV) and Ni 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (875.0&nbsp;eV) with two Sat. peaks,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0042_R_d39507008e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (935.8&nbsp;eV) and Cu 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (956.7&nbsp;eV),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0043_R_d39507008e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Mater. Chem. A">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> validating that Mn, Co, Ni, and Cu exist in the form of bivalent ions.</p>
            
            <p>As shown from low-magnification panoramic field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM) images (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0002">2a,b</a>), the resulted NVPOF-HE, compared to the counterpart without HE doping (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), still shows typical nanoparticle morphology, however, its size become as small as ≈35&nbsp;nm, as depicted in the particle size distribution diagram (the inset in panel b), suggesting the essential role of HE doping in particle refinement. More interestingly to note is that such phenomenon is observed for the cases of NVPOF-C (≈140&nbsp;nm, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a,b</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-CC (≈65&nbsp;nm, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12c,d</a>, Supporting Information) and NVPOF-CCN (≈47&nbsp;nm, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12e,f</a>, Supporting Information). With the detailed discussions above in mind, it is easy to draw a conclusion that the more doped metal ions, the smaller the particle size, that is, the particle size refinement effect turns out to be increasingly distinct with the increase in types of doped hetero-ions, which can be corroborated visually by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) observations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a–c</a>, Supporting Information). As is well established, the smaller size favors for shortened diffusion path of sodium ions, enhancing electrochemical kinetics for efficient sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0032_R_d39507008e1045" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Eng. J.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typical high-resolution TEM image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0002">2d</a>) and the different intensity profile (the inset in panel d) apparently display the well-discerned lattice fringes with a spacing of ≈0.569&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the NVPOF (002) crystal face, somewhat larger than the phase-pure NVPOF (≈0.529&nbsp;nm) due to HE doping. Besides, the enlarged spacings of lattice fringes are apparent for the NVPOF-C (≈0.521&nbsp;nm, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13d</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-CC (≈0.542&nbsp;nm, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13e</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-CCN (≈0.552&nbsp;nm, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13f</a>, Supporting Information), owing to the noticeable difference in doping ions. Corresponding energy dispersive X-ray (EDX) elemental mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0002">2e</a>) starkly visualize the even distributions of all the doped Cr, Co, Cu, Mn and Ni in the nano-phase NVPOF grains.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403282-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a729c581-19a9-4703-9e89-ae2cd308a1f4/aenm202403282-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a729c581-19a9-4703-9e89-ae2cd308a1f4/aenm202403282-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1203de8-5d81-4b4f-b7d8-ad8b94693c73/aenm202403282-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a729c581-19a9-4703-9e89-ae2cd308a1f4/aenm202403282-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403282-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403282" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) FESEM images, size distribution diagram (the inset in panel b), c) TEM image, d) HTERM image and corresponding FFT analysis (the inset in panel d), e) scanning TEM and corresponding elemental (Na, O, F, Cr, Co, V, P, Cu, Mn and Ni) EDX mapping images of NVPOF-HE.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403282-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="aenm202403282-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Evaluation of NVPOF-HE in Half Cells</h3>
            
            <p>In order to gain a better understanding of electrochemical performance of NVPOF-HE, metallic Na was used as the reference and counter electrode in a half cell. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0003">3a</a> comparatively shows the CV curves of the five cathodes at a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> with a potential range of 2.5–4.5&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Notably, two pairs of oxidation (A1, A2) and reduction (C1, C2) peaks can be observed in all five cases, which are attributed to the redox pairs of V<sup>5+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0026_R_d39507008e1113" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to other four counterparts, the two pairs of redox peaks of NVPOF-HE are even more sharp and symmetrical. Furthermore, the modest polarization, as examined from the potential gaps of A1/C1 (∆V<sub>1</sub>, ≈52&nbsp;mV) and A2/C2 (∆V<sub>2</sub>, ≈38&nbsp;mV) redox peaks, are apparent, which are obviously smaller than those of other four counterpart cathodes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), fully evidencing the best electrochemical kinetics and reversibility of the NVPOF-HE cathode. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0003">3b</a> profiles the initial charge-discharge plots of the five different cathodes as indicated at 0.5 C (1 C = 130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). All the electrodes clearly manifest two charge/discharge platforms contributed by the reversible transition of the involved electrochemical species of V<sup>5+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup>/V<sup>3+</sup>. Note that the first discharging capacity of NVPOF is only 105.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is seriously lower than the single-ion (NVPOF-C, 116.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), two-ion (NVPOF-CC, 121 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and three-ion (NVPOF-CCN, 122.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) doped cases. More encouragingly, the first-loop discharge capacity of NVPOF-HE is as high as 126.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is close to the theoretical capacity. Considering the doped ion-dependent potential gaps and discharge capacities, as mentioned above, it is easy to conclude that more ion doping favors the promotion in sodium-storage behaviors, particularly highlighting the essential role of the HE doping strategy. Beyond this, the five different cathodes further demonstrate different capacity contributions within the specific voltage ranges, as profiled in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0003">3c</a>. The capacity contribution ratios of NVPOF-HE in the low-voltage (&lt;3.5&nbsp;V), medium-voltage (3.5–3.8&nbsp;V), and high-voltage (&gt;3.8&nbsp;V) ranges are estimated as 3.8%, 44.6%, and 51.6%, respectively. In contrast, the increased capacity contributions in the low-voltage region are apparent for NVPOF (18.7%), NVPOF-C (11.1%), NVPOF-CC (5.0) and NVPOF-CCN (5.3%), which means the reduced capacity contribution especially under high operation voltages occurring in the four cathodes. The capacity contribution ratios in the medium/high voltage region are higher than that of NVPOF (34.8%/46.5%), NVPOF-C (41.3%/47.6%), NVPOF-CC (49.7%/45.3%), and NVPOF-CCN (51.1%/43.6%). Therefore, the appealing HE effect can effectively improve the capacity contribution proportions in medium/high voltage regions, which indeed benefits for the enhancement in device-level energy density.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403282-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/67aa170c-e090-4472-b6f5-7dcd5ad691a3/aenm202403282-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/67aa170c-e090-4472-b6f5-7dcd5ad691a3/aenm202403282-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eb91ea53-0f21-4a5c-a0ee-2e610b935fd3/aenm202403282-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/67aa170c-e090-4472-b6f5-7dcd5ad691a3/aenm202403282-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403282-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403282" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of half cells: a) CV curves (0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>), b) charge/discharge profiles at 0.5 C, and c) corresponding capacity contribution at different discharge voltage ranges. d) Rate performance at different rates, e) charge/discharge profiles for NVPOF-HE at different scan rates, and f) comparison in rate capability of NVPOF-HE with other reported cathode materials. g) Cycling capabilities at 1C, and h) long-term cycle stability at 10 C of the five cathodes as indicated.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0003">3d</a> manifests the high-rate performance of the five specimens from 0.5 to 50 C. On the whole, the discharge capacities of the NVPOF-HE are significantly higher than those of other four electrodes at all C rates. Particularly, the NVPOF electrode just provides a discharge capacity of 12.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 C, that is, the capacity retention rate at 50 C (relative to 0.5 C) is only ≈12.1%. By contrast, the NVPOF-C, NVPOF-CC, and NVPOF-CCN provide larger discharge capacities of 22.1, 37.5, and 60.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, at 50 C rate, thanks to the positive contribution of hetero-atom doping. More impressively, besides the highest reversible capacity of 126.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5 C, NVPOF-HE also obtains a discharge capacity of 73.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a high-magnification rate of 50 C, which corresponds to a capacity decay rate of ≈41.5% with a 100-fold increase in C rates. Moreover, with the charge-discharge rate returning to 0.5 C, NVPOF-HE still can achieve a discharge capacity of 125.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, indicative of its excellent reversibility under different C rates. As can be seen from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0003">3e</a>, with the increase of the magnification in C rate, the NVPOF-HE electrode still has a relatively obvious stepped voltage platforms along with a gradual increase in polarization degree. While the discharge–discharge platforms of NVPOF-CCN (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-CC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14c</a>, Supporting Information) and NVPOF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14d</a>, Supporting Information) cathodes significantly decrease with the increase in C rates, and even vanish at an ultra-high rate of 50 C, especially for the case of NVPOF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14d</a>, Supporting Information). The sluggish kinetics makes the four cathodes unable to carry out deep redox reaction at such large magnification rates. More encouragingly, the exceptional rate properties of NVPOF-HE, as intuitively observed in the capacity-rate (C) diagram (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0003">3f</a>), is better and/or competitive to other reported state-of-the-art NVPOF cathodes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0015_R_d39507008e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0044_R_d39507008e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">44</a>-<a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0047_R_d39507008e1213" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Energy Storage Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> particularly in terms of high C rates.</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0003">3g</a> shows the cyclic performance of the five cathode materials at 1 C rate. Notably, the NVPOF-HE delivers the largest initial discharge capacity of 120.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> among the electrodes. Besides this, after 200 charge-discharge cycles, NVPOF-HE can still maintain a reversible capacity of up to 119.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention rate of 99.6%, that is, a decrease of only 0.002% per cycle, which is in deep contrast with other cathodes. Specifically, the capacity retentions of NVPOF, NVPOF-C, NVPOF-CC, and NVPOF-CCN are estimated as ≈82.3% (85.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), ≈88.6% (98.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), ≈96.9% (108.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and ≈94.5% (108.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. More importantly, by comparing the charge and discharge plots of the five samples from the 1st to the 200th circle at 1 C, it is apparent that NVPOF-HE always has similar charge and discharge shapes and the curves over cycling are highly overlapped along with the well-remained voltage platforms (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a>, Supporting Information). However, the voltage platforms of NVPOF-CCN (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b</a>, Supporting Information) and NVPOF-CC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15c</a>, Supporting Information) show more or less polarization along with the capacity loss with the deepening of the cycle. In particular, NVPOF-C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15d</a>, Supporting Information) and NVPOF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15e</a>, Supporting Information) show an extremely significant voltage drop as the cycle progresses. More encouragingly, even under an ultra-high 10 C rate (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0003">3h</a>), just a capacity degradation of ≈9.8% is gained by NVPOF-HE after 4000 consecutive cycles, which is equivalent to a capacity reduction of ≈0.0002% per charging-discharge cycle, while maintaining a high Coulombic efficiency close to 100%. As regards NVPOF-CCN and NVPOF-CC, the discharge capacities of 91.3 and 64.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> still can be retained after the same cycles, corresponding to the capacity retentions of ≈71.2% and 76.1%, respectively. More seriously, both the NVPOF-C and NVPOF cathodes almost completely fail, and huge capacity decay rates of ≈55.0% and 70.2% are estimated for the two, respectively. To further verify the cycling stability of NVPOF-HE, the current density is further increased to 30 C, and after 6500 cycles, the capacity retention rate is still as high as 94.1% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). More strikingly, the Na<sup>+</sup>-storage properties in terms of both high-rate capacity and cycling stability of NVPOF-HE are superior to other existing NVPOF cathodes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Such impressive results feature that NVPOF-HE holds the enormous potential to be a positive electrode material for the next-generation SIBs.</p>
            
            <p>In order to gain in-depth understandings of electrochemical reaction kinetics associated with the HE strategy, CV tests were first performed at different scanning rates of 0.1−1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The underlying Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion process of the five electrodes can be reflected through the linear relationship between the square root of the scan rate and the peak current. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0004">4a</a> displays representative CV curves of NVPOF-HE under various rates as indicated. As noted, under all these sweep rates, its two pairs of redox peaks are always clear, sharp, and symmetrical, and just small redox peak gaps (∆V<sub>1</sub>, ≈138; ∆V<sub>2</sub>, ≈97&nbsp;mV) can be found with the scanning rate is up to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> from a small rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The polarization degree of NVPOF-HE here is even smaller than other four counterparts including NVPOF-CCN (∆V<sub>1</sub>, ≈167&nbsp;mV; ∆V<sub>2</sub>, ≈132&nbsp;mV, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17a</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-CC (∆V<sub>1</sub>, ≈183&nbsp;mV; ∆V<sub>2</sub>, ≈166&nbsp;mV, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17c</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-C (∆V<sub>1</sub>, ≈224&nbsp;mV; ∆V<sub>2</sub>, ≈172&nbsp;mV, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17e</a>, Supporting Information) and NVPOF (∆V<sub>1</sub>, ≈426&nbsp;mV; ∆V<sub>2</sub>, ≈363&nbsp;mV, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0004">4b</a>), indicating the fastest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of NVPOF-HE. According to the classic Randles-Sevcik equation:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0048_R_d39507008e1320" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Small">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <i>I<sub>p</sub></i> = 2.69 × 10<sup>5</sup> <i>n</i><sup>3/2</sup><i>AD</i><sup>1/2</sup><i>C</i>ν<sup>1/2</sup>, where <i>n</i>, <i>A</i>, <i>D</i>, and C represent the number of electrons, the effective working area (cm<sup>2</sup>), the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>), and the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> (mol cm<sup>−3</sup>), respectively, generally, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion process is positively correlated with the peak slope of <i>I<sub>p</sub></i> and <i>ν</i><sup>1/2</sup>. As plotted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0004">4c</a>, the peak slopes of A1, A2, C1, and C2 are ≈0.92, 1.41, −0.82, and −1.37, respectively, for NVPOF-HE, even higher than those of NVPOF-CCN (0.79, 1.21, −0.79, and −1.05; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17b</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-CC (0.73, 1.28, −0.74, and −1.26 Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17d</a>, Supporting Information), NVPOF-C (0.69, 0.87, −0.72, and −0.74; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17f</a>, Supporting Information), and NVPOF (0.61, 0.96, −0.49, and −0.70; Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0004">4d</a>) cathodes, which further certificates that the improved diffusion kinetics results from a HE-doping strategy indeed.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403282-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/eb914e52-25ff-436a-b602-2b1165b36f8f/aenm202403282-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/eb914e52-25ff-436a-b602-2b1165b36f8f/aenm202403282-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a2ad7c79-f99c-4fbd-9c02-4027f1489785/aenm202403282-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/eb914e52-25ff-436a-b602-2b1165b36f8f/aenm202403282-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403282-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403282" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) CV curves at various scanning rates as indicated and c,d) corresponding relationships between peak current (<i>I<sub>p</sub></i>) and square root of scan rates (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>): a,c) NVPOF-HE; b,d) NVPOF. e) Nyquist plots after 100 cycles at 10 C and corresponding f) liner fitting of <i>Z`</i> versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> in the low-frequency region for the five cathodes as indicated.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0004">4e</a> depicts the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) data of the five cathodes after 50 cycles at 1 C and charged to 4.0&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Typically, the high/mid-frequency semicircles are related to the electrode-electrolyte interface charge transfer resistance (<i>R<sub>ct</sub></i>), and the linear trials in low frequency region reflect the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion resistance (i.e., Warburg resistance, W). With the proposed equivalent circuit diagram (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information), the <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> values of NVPOF-HE, NVPOF-CCN, NVPOF-CC, NVPOF-C and NVPOF are fitted as 65.3, 108.7, 133.9, 176.4, and 355.9 Ω, respectively. In addition, Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D<sub>EIS, Na+</sub></i>) can be evaluated according to the following formula:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0049_R_d39507008e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Energy Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <i>D<sub>EIS, Na+</sub></i> = <i>R</i><sup>2</sup><i>T</i><sup>2</sup>/(2<i>A</i><sup>2</sup><i>n</i><sup>4</sup><i>F</i><sup>4</sup><i>C</i><sup>2</sup><i>σ</i><sup>2</sup>), where <i>R</i>, <i>T</i>, <i>A</i>, <i>n</i>, <i>F</i>, C, <i>σ</i> correspond to the gas constant, temperature, effective work area, number of charged electrons, Faraday's constant, ion concentration, Warburg coefficient, respectively. Classically, the <i>D<sub>EIS, Na+</sub></i>, and <i>σ</i> are negatively correlated. According to the slope fitted by the linear relationship between the corresponding low-frequency region <i>Z`</i> and <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup>, as demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0004">4f</a>, the <i>σ</i> values of NVPOF-HE, NVPOF-CCN, NVPOF-CC, NVPOF-C, and NVPOF are obtained as 1.9, 5.3, 12.4, 15.1, and 17.7, respectively. It is further proved from another perspective that the HE doping advances the Na<sup>+</sup>-transport kinetics.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was introduced as well to reveal the microscopic effect of the HE doping strategy on Na<sup>+</sup> storage dynamics of NVPOF-HE. It can be seen that NVPOF-HE has a larger titration time than NVPOF, and the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D<sub>GITT, Na+</sub></i>) of the two samples both render the minimum values ≈3.6 and 4.0&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), which corresponds to the oxidation and reduction platforms of the electrodes due to the slow redox action of the V-center atoms. The <i>D<sub>GITT, Na+</sub></i> values of the NVPOF in the charge and discharge process are in the order of ≈10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (the inset in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information), however, the diffusion coefficients of NVPOF-HE in these two regions are on the order of ≈10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (the inset in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19b</a>, Supporting Information), which is ≈2 orders of magnitude higher than that of NVPOF. In addition, with the potential higher than 4.1&nbsp;V, the <i>D<sub>GITT, Na+</sub></i> of NVPOF rapidly drops to the order of 10<sup>−12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which may be due to the increase of defect formation energy caused by strong Na ordering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0015_R_d39507008e1554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while that of NVPOF-HE in this region is basically stable. The observation here authenticates that the HE strategy greatly inhibits the increase of defect formation energy, activates the activity of V-central site, realizing rapid reaction kinetics, which will be conducive to the improvement in rate performance and structural stability of NVPOF-HE.</p>
            
            <p>In order to further figure out the detailed structural evolution of NVPOF during charge and discharge processes, the in situ XRD tests were conducted (2<i>θ</i> = 10–40°) on both the NVPOF-HE and NVPOF in the voltage range of 2.5−4.5&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) at 0.3 C, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0005">5a–f</a>. Obviously, both the two electrodes present similar variation in diffraction peaks during charge and discharge, revealing the similar Na<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanism of the two. In the original state, the characteristic reflections of NVPOF-HE corresponding to (101), (002), (200), (103), and (202) crystal faces in the 2<i>θ</i> range are evident, and their evolution during the charge and discharge phase is evaluated accordingly. During the charging process, the peak intensity of (200) at 2<i>θ</i> = 27.26&nbsp;° gradually weakens, along with a new peak appearing at 2<i>θ</i> = 28.53°. And at the end of the first charging platform, the (200) peak completely vanishes and form a new Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F phase, indicating that an Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted from NVPOF at this stage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0050_R_d39507008e1594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Mater.">50</a>, <a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0051_R_d39507008e1594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With further charging, the newly generated peak gradually merges with the (103) peak to form a new peak at 2<i>θ</i> = 29.11° after completing the second charging platform. Similarly, the generation of new peaks and the merging of peaks can also be observed in peak pairs of (101) and (002). For instance, the peaks of the two planes (101) and (002) also merge to form a new diffraction peak at 2<i>θ</i> = 16.64° when the second charging platform is completed. In addition, the position of the diffraction peak (202) shifts slightly to the right during charging. These phase changes accompanying the second charging platform mean that another Na<sup>+</sup> ion is extracted from Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F to produce the NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>F phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0050_R_d39507008e1626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Mater.">50</a>-<a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0052_R_d39507008e1626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is especially worth of mentioning that when discharged to 2.5&nbsp;V, all the diffraction peaks of the two cathodes can be restored again to the initial state, which reveals that a highly reversible transition has taken place between the three phases, as schematically described in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0005">5g</a>. Moreover, to compare the volumetric changes in the crystal structure during the sodium insertion/desertion process of NVPOF-HE and NVPOF, the <i>c</i>-axis is taken as an example to record its detailed change with the charging and discharging potential, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0005">5h</a>. Evidently, the <i>c</i>-axis of NVPOF-HE constantly decreases in its lattice constant owing to the withdrawal of sodium ions during the charging process. At the two charging platforms (i.e., 3.6 and 4.0&nbsp;V), the decrease is particularly distinct, but the <i>c</i>-axis change rate (∆<i>c</i>) is only ≈−0.8% for NVPOF-HE. In addition to the <i>c</i>-axis shrinkage at the two platforms, NVPOF shrinks again when the voltage reaches 4.5&nbsp;V, indicating that the cathode releases sodium ions at the inactive site at higher potentials, causing damage to the material structure, which corresponding to the aforementioned GITT result (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19a</a>, Supporting Information), and its ∆<i>c</i> reaches ≈−1.3%. Therefore, even though the two cathodes possess similar charge-discharge mechanisms, the HE avenue significantly improves the interface stability of the materials, broadens the sodium ion diffusion channel, reinforces the crystalline structure, and buffers structural deformation of NVPOF-HE during the charge and discharge processes, so as to realize the high-structure-stability requirement for long-duration yet reversible two-electron-reaction cycles particularly under high voltages.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403282-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cfef8d9f-1f0a-435f-aea2-937a96d7d205/aenm202403282-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/cfef8d9f-1f0a-435f-aea2-937a96d7d205/aenm202403282-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/be0cf5d8-1585-484a-80e1-9e0da1d469ba/aenm202403282-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cfef8d9f-1f0a-435f-aea2-937a96d7d205/aenm202403282-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403282-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403282" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In situ XRD patterns, corresponding contours, and voltage–time curves of a–c) NVPOF-HE and d–f) NVPOF in different states. g) A schematic diagram for the crystal structure transition of NVPOF-HE over charging/discharging. h) The change in lattice constant of <i>c</i> during charge and discharge processes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In order to understand the effects of the HE doping, first-principles calculation was performed to understand the effects of HE doping on the migration of Na<sup>+</sup>. Electronic structure of materials is greatly vital for understanding their electronic conductivity properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0053_R_d39507008e1680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Energy Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The density of states (DOS) of pristine and doped NVPOF are calculated to illustrate the variation of electronic structures between pristine and doped NVPOF, especially the change of band gaps. The total DOS curves obtained from the NVPOF calculations are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0006">6a</a>. Compared with the pristine NVPOF, the band gap of NVPOF-C, NVPOF-CC, NVPOF-CCN, and NVPOF-HE decrease sequentially, among which the NVPOF-HE shows a significant decrease in band gap, which indicates the enhancement of HE effect on the electronic conductivity of NVPOF. In another hand, to further understand the effect of HE on NVPOF, Na<sup>+</sup> migration energy barrier calculations are performed for pristine and doped NVPOF. The optimized configuration of both pristine and NVPOF-HE are plotted (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a,b</a>, Supporting Information), and the migration path of Na(2) → Na(1) in the original NVPOF structure is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0006">6b</a>. And the same path is chosen for the redesigned NVPOF-HE (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0006">6c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0054_R_d39507008e1703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a>, <a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0055_R_d39507008e1703" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Nano">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As illustrated in the migration states of Na<sup>+</sup> in the pristine and in NVPOF-HE (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a–f</a>, Supporting Information), the IS, TS, and FS represent the initial state, transition state, and final state, respectively. Similarly, the migration paths of Na<sup>+</sup> in NVPOF-CCN, NVPOF-CC, and NVPOF-C are also explored, with the IS, TS, and FS states illustrated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information). Using the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method, the migration energy barriers of NVPOF, NVPOF-C, NVPOF-CC, NVPOF-CCN and NVPOF-HE were also calculated, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403282-fig-0006">6d–h</a>, with the results being 1.0271, 0.9797, 0.9269, 0.7648, and 0.6686&nbsp;eV, respectively. Among them, the energy of the FS in NVPOF-HE (0.3566&nbsp;eV) is much lower than that of NVPOF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0056_R_d39507008e1723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Phys. Rev. B">56</a>, <a href="#aenm202403282-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403282-bib-0057_R_d39507008e1723" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Mater. Chem. A">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is clear to see that the migration energy barrier declines obviously with the increase of doping, with especially a cutdown of up to 35% in NVPOF-HE when compared to the case of NVPOF, indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup> in NVPOF-HE is much easier to escape and it is hugely benefit to the rearrangement of Na<sup>+</sup> site. Moreover, considering the low doping concentrations in the experiments, the independent effects of each dopant element on the sodium migration energy barriers was also calculated separately (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). As seen from all the optimized structures and migration pathways (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information), even when individual atoms independently influence the sodium migration pathway, the migration energy barriers are significantly reduced, but not as pronounced as those with HE doping. Overall, the reduced migration barrier and band gap induced by the HE effect demonstrate the evident advantage in reaction kinetics and electronic conductivity of NVPOF-HE for efficient sodium storage.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403282-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f713338f-5adb-41d1-a9c1-cd82434c9432/aenm202403282-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f713338f-5adb-41d1-a9c1-cd82434c9432/aenm202403282-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c50c9978-e414-4adf-8ccc-6dc742ae3352/aenm202403282-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f713338f-5adb-41d1-a9c1-cd82434c9432/aenm202403282-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403282-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403282" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
